**Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing an amino acid structure with a carboxylic acid group (e.g., (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, (2S)-2-aminobutanedioic acid) tend to result in high yield. This is likely due to the ability of amino acids to coordinate with metal centers, enhancing the catalytic activity.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group directly attached to a carboxylic acid (e.g., 4-aminobenzoic acid, 4-nitrobenzoic acid) generally lead to high yield. The aromatic ring may facilitate π-π interactions with the catalyst, stabilizing the active site.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a hydroxyl group in conjunction with a carboxylic acid (e.g., 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid, 2-oxo-1H-pyridine-4-carboxylic acid) are associated with high yield. The hydroxyl group can enhance solubility and interaction with the catalyst.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a sulfur-containing group, such as thiol or sulfanyl (e.g., 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid, 4-sulfanylbenzoic acid), tend to result in high yield. Sulfur can act as a soft base, coordinating well with transition metals like Fe.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with multiple ether linkages (e.g., 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid) tend to result in low yield. The ether groups may interfere with the coordination of the modifier to the metal center, reducing catalytic efficiency.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with a high degree of fluorination (e.g., 2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid) generally lead to low yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of fluorine can destabilize the metal-ligand interaction.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with an indole or pyridine ring (e.g., (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid, pyridine-3-carboxylic acid) tend to result in high yield. These heterocycles can coordinate with the metal center, enhancing catalytic activity.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with formyl groups (e.g., 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid, 4-formylbenzoic acid) are associated with low yield. The formyl group may lead to competing side reactions or deactivate the catalyst.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with a simple aliphatic chain and a terminal carboxylic acid (e.g., octanoic acid, 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid) tend to result in low yield. The lack of additional functional groups may limit interactions with the catalyst.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with both an amino and a carboxylic acid group but lacking aromatic or heterocyclic structures (e.g., 8-aminooctanoic acid, 7-aminoheptanoic acid) generally lead to low yield. The absence of aromatic stabilization may reduce the effectiveness of metal coordination.

4. **Train Leverage**: The current train leverage is 0.0078, which is positive and an improvement over the previous negative leverage values. Positive leverage indicates that the rule is capturing associations that are stronger than what would be expected by chance, which is a positive development.

**Comments and Suggestions for Improvement:**

- **Broaden Rule Applicability**: The low train support suggests that the rules might be too specific. Consider revising the rules to capture a broader range of conditions that still maintain high confidence and lift. This could involve relaxing some constraints or combining similar rules to increase coverage.

- **Maintain High Confidence and Lift**: The improvements in confidence and lift are positive, indicating that the rules are becoming more reliable and informative. Efforts should be made to maintain these metrics while increasing support.

- **Explore Additional Features**: Consider incorporating additional features or modifying existing ones to enhance the rules' ability to capture relevant patterns. This could involve exploring different chemical structures or functional groups that might influence catalytic performance.

- **Iterative Refinement**: Continue iterating on the rules, using the current metrics as a guide. Aim to find a balance between specificity and generality to ensure that the rules are both applicable and informative.

Overall, the current iteration shows promising improvements in confidence and lift, but there is room to increase support to ensure the rules are applicable to a larger portion of the dataset.

ML Commenter Message:
### Performance Evaluation of Current Rules

1. **Effectiveness of Current Rules:**
   - The current validation accuracy of 0.6286 is significantly lower than the previous reference accuracies, which were consistently around 0.76. This suggests that the current rules may not be as effective in capturing the underlying patterns in the data.

2. **Overfitting/Underfitting Assessment:**
   - The drop in validation accuracy indicates potential underfitting, where the model is not capturing the complexity of the data. This could be due to overly simplistic rules or a lack of diversity in the feature set.

3. **Discrepancies Analysis:**
   - For molecules where the predicted loading is high but the experimental value is low, Rule 1 and Rule 11 often have high positive SHAP values. This suggests these rules might be overestimating the loading.
   - Conversely, for molecules where the predicted loading is low but the experimental value is high, Rule 8 and Rule 11 frequently have high negative SHAP values, indicating these rules might be underestimating the loading.

4. **Accuracy Improvements:**
   - The current accuracy metrics suggest that there is room for improvement. The significant drop from previous accuracies indicates that the current iteration of rules may not be capturing the necessary features or interactions effectively.

5. **Insights from SHAP Analysis:**
   - SHAP analysis highlights that Rule 1 and Rule 11 are influential in both overestimations and underestimations. This suggests that these rules might be too broad or not specific enough, leading to inconsistent predictions.
   - Rule 8, which often has a negative SHAP value, might be too restrictive, potentially missing cases where the modifier could still result in high loading despite the presence of formyl groups.

### Recommendations for Improvement

1. **Refine Rules with High Positive SHAP Values:**
   - **Rule 1:** Consider adding more specific conditions or exceptions to Rule 1 to prevent overestimation. For example, refine the types of aromatic or heterocyclic rings that truly enhance coordination.
   - **Rule 11:** Re-evaluate the impact of electron-withdrawing groups. It might be beneficial to specify which groups enhance coordination without destabilizing the metal-ligand interaction.

2. **Adjust Rules with High Negative SHAP Values:**
   - **Rule 8:** Reassess the impact of formyl groups. Consider scenarios where formyl groups might not lead to low yield, such as when other stabilizing groups are present.
   - **Rule 11:** As it appears in both overestimation and underestimation, this rule might need a more nuanced approach, possibly splitting it into sub-rules based on different electron-withdrawing groups.

3. **Improve Generalization:**
   - Introduce additional features or rules that capture interactions not currently considered, such as steric effects or specific metal-modifier interactions.
   - Consider using a more diverse training set to ensure the rules are not overly tailored to a specific subset of data.

4. **Iterative Refinement:**
   - Use insights from SHAP analysis to iteratively test and refine rules. For instance, conduct experiments to validate the impact of specific functional groups or molecular structures highlighted by SHAP values.
   - Continuously evaluate the impact of rule adjustments on both validation and test accuracies to ensure improvements are generalizable.

By addressing these areas, the rules can be refined to better capture the complexity of the data, potentially improving both validation and test accuracies.

The combination of amino acids and aromatic rings enhances coordination with metal centers and stabilizes the catalytic site.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group directly attached to a carboxylic acid and an additional electron-donating group (e.g., 4-aminobenzoic acid, 4-nitrobenzoic acid) generally lead to high yield. The aromatic ring facilitates π-π interactions, while electron-donating groups enhance the electron density around the metal center.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a hydroxyl group in conjunction with a carboxylic acid and an additional aromatic ring (e.g., 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid) are associated with high yield. The hydroxyl group enhances solubility and interaction with the catalyst, while the aromatic ring provides additional stabilization.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a sulfur-containing group, such as thiol or sulfanyl, and an aromatic ring (e.g., 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid) tend to result in high yield. Sulfur acts as a soft base, coordinating well with transition metals, and the aromatic ring stabilizes the active site.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with multiple ether linkages and no additional functional groups (e.g., 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid) tend to result in low yield. The ether groups may interfere with the coordination of the modifier to the metal center, reducing catalytic efficiency.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with a high degree of fluorination and no additional coordinating groups (e.g., 2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid) generally lead to low yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of fluorine can destabilize the metal-ligand interaction.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with an indole or pyridine ring and an additional coordinating group (e.g., pyridine-3-carboxylic acid) tend to result in high yield. These heterocycles can coordinate with the metal center, enhancing catalytic activity.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with formyl groups and no additional stabilizing groups (e.g., 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid) are associated with low yield. The formyl group may lead to competing side reactions or deactivate the catalyst.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with a simple aliphatic chain and a terminal carboxylic acid, lacking additional functional groups (e.g., octanoic acid), tend to result in low yield. The lack of additional functional groups may limit interactions with the catalyst.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with both an amino and a carboxylic acid group but lacking aromatic or heterocyclic structures (e.g., 8-aminooctanoic acid) generally lead to low yield. The absence of aromatic stabilization may reduce the effectiveness of metal coordination.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group and an electron-withdrawing group such as a nitro or halogen (e.g., 4-nitrobenzoic acid) tend to result in high yield. The electron-withdrawing group can enhance the acidity of the carboxylic acid, improving coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and a carboxylic acid group (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid) tend to result in low yield if there are no additional stabilizing groups. The lack of additional stabilizing groups may lead to insufficient coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a benzofuran structure and a carboxylic acid group (e.g., 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid) tend to result in high yield. The benzofuran structure can provide additional π-π interactions and stabilization.

Rule Generator Message:
**Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing an amino acid structure with a carboxylic acid group and an aromatic ring, specifically phenyl or indole (e.g., (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid), tend to result in high yield. These structures enhance coordination with metal centers and stabilize the catalytic site through π-π interactions.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group directly attached to a carboxylic acid and an electron-donating group such as amino or hydroxyl (e.g., 4-aminobenzoic acid, 4-hydroxybenzoic acid) generally lead to high yield. The electron-donating groups increase electron density around the metal center, enhancing catalytic activity.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a hydroxyl group in conjunction with a carboxylic acid and an additional aromatic ring (e.g., 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid) are associated with high yield. The hydroxyl group enhances solubility and interaction with the catalyst, while the aromatic ring provides additional stabilization.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a sulfur-containing group, such as thiol or sulfanyl, and an aromatic ring (e.g., 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid) tend to result in high yield. Sulfur acts as a soft base, coordinating well with transition metals, and the aromatic ring stabilizes the active site.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with multiple ether linkages and no additional functional groups (e.g., 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid) tend to result in low yield. The ether groups may interfere with the coordination of the modifier to the metal center, reducing catalytic efficiency.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with a high degree of fluorination and no additional coordinating groups (e.g., 2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid) generally lead to low yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of fluorine can destabilize the metal-ligand interaction.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with an indole or pyridine ring and an additional coordinating group (e.g., pyridine-3-carboxylic acid) tend to result in high yield. These heterocycles can coordinate with the metal center, enhancing catalytic activity.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with formyl groups and no additional stabilizing groups (e.g., 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid) are associated with low yield. The formyl group may lead to competing side reactions or deactivate the catalyst.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with a simple aliphatic chain and a terminal carboxylic acid, lacking additional functional groups (e.g., octanoic acid), tend to result in low yield. The lack of additional functional groups may limit interactions with the catalyst.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with both an amino and a carboxylic acid group but lacking aromatic or heterocyclic structures (e.g., 8-aminooctanoic acid) generally lead to low yield. The absence of aromatic stabilization may reduce the effectiveness of metal coordination.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group and an electron-withdrawing group such as a nitro group (e.g., 4-nitrobenzoic acid) tend to result in high yield. The electron-withdrawing group can enhance the acidity of the carboxylic acid, improving coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and a carboxylic acid group (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid) tend to result in low yield if there are no additional stabilizing groups. The lack of additional stabilizing groups may lead to insufficient coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a benzofuran structure and a carboxylic acid group (e.g., 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid) tend to result in high yield. The benzofuran structure can provide additional π-π interactions and stabilization.

**End of Rules**

    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing an amino acid structure with a carboxylic acid group and an aromatic ring, specifically phenyl or indole (e.g., (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid), tend to result in high yield. These structures enhance coordination with metal centers and stabilize the catalytic site through π-π interactions.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group directly attached to a carboxylic acid and an electron-donating group such as amino or hydroxyl (e.g., 4-aminobenzoic acid, 4-hydroxybenzoic acid) generally lead to high yield. The electron-donating groups increase electron density around the metal center, enhancing catalytic activity.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a hydroxyl group in conjunction with a carboxylic acid and an additional aromatic ring (e.g., 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid) are associated with high yield. The hydroxyl group enhances solubility and interaction with the catalyst, while the aromatic ring provides additional stabilization.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a sulfur-containing group, such as thiol or sulfanyl, and an aromatic ring (e.g., 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid) tend to result in high yield. Sulfur acts as a soft base, coordinating well with transition metals, and the aromatic ring stabilizes the active site.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with multiple ether linkages and no additional functional groups (e.g., 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid) tend to result in low yield. The ether groups may interfere with the coordination of the modifier to the metal center, reducing catalytic efficiency.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with a high degree of fluorination and no additional coordinating groups (e.g., 2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid) generally lead to low yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of fluorine can destabilize the metal-ligand interaction.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with an indole or pyridine ring and an additional coordinating group (e.g., pyridine-3-carboxylic acid) tend to result in high yield. These heterocycles can coordinate with the metal center, enhancing catalytic activity.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with formyl groups and no additional stabilizing groups (e.g., 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid) are associated with low yield. The formyl group may lead to competing side reactions or deactivate the catalyst.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with a simple aliphatic chain and a terminal carboxylic acid, lacking additional functional groups (e.g., octanoic acid), tend to result in low yield. The lack of additional functional groups may limit interactions with the catalyst.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with both an amino and a carboxylic acid group but lacking aromatic or heterocyclic structures (e.g., 8-aminooctanoic acid) generally lead to low yield. The absence of aromatic stabilization may reduce the effectiveness of metal coordination.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group and an electron-withdrawing group such as a nitro group (e.g., 4-nitrobenzoic acid) tend to result in high yield. The electron-withdrawing group can enhance the acidity of the carboxylic acid, improving coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and a carboxylic acid group (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid) tend to result in low yield if there are no additional stabilizing groups. The lack of additional stabilizing groups may lead to insufficient coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a benzofuran structure and a carboxylic acid group (e.g., 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid) tend to result in high yield. The benzofuran structure can provide additional π-π interactions and stabilization.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group and an electron-withdrawing group such as a nitro group (e.g., 4-nitrobenzoic acid) tend to result in high yield. The electron-withdrawing group can enhance the acidity of the carboxylic acid, improving coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and a carboxylic acid group (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid) tend to result in low yield if there are no additional stabilizing groups. The lack of additional stabilizing groups may lead to insufficient coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a benzofuran structure and a carboxylic acid group (e.g., 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid) tend to result in high yield. The benzofuran structure can provide additional π-π interactions and stabilization.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and an electron-donating group such as an amino group (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid) tend to result in high yield. The electron-donating group enhances coordination with the metal center, increasing catalytic efficiency.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group and a methoxy group on an aromatic ring (e.g., 4-(3,4-dimethoxyphenyl)-4-oxobutanoic acid) tend to result in high yield. The methoxy group can enhance electron density and stabilization through resonance.

    Current_Rules:
    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing an amino acid structure with a carboxylic acid group and an aromatic ring, specifically phenyl or indole (e.g., (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid), tend to result in high yield. These structures enhance coordination with metal centers and stabilize the catalytic site through π-π interactions.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group directly attached to a carboxylic acid and an electron-donating group such as amino or hydroxyl (e.g., 4-aminobenzoic acid, 4-hydroxybenzoic acid) generally lead to high yield. The electron-donating groups increase electron density around the metal center, enhancing catalytic activity.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a hydroxyl group in conjunction with a carboxylic acid and an additional aromatic ring (e.g., 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid) are associated with high yield. The hydroxyl group enhances solubility and interaction with the catalyst, while the aromatic ring provides additional stabilization.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a sulfur-containing group, such as thiol or sulfanyl, and an aromatic ring (e.g., 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid) tend to result in high yield. Sulfur acts as a soft base, coordinating well with transition metals, and the aromatic ring stabilizes the active site.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with multiple ether linkages and no additional functional groups (e.g., 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid) tend to result in low yield. The ether groups may interfere with the coordination of the modifier to the metal center, reducing catalytic efficiency.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with a high degree of fluorination and no additional coordinating groups (e.g., 2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid) generally lead to low yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of fluorine can destabilize the metal-ligand interaction.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with an indole or pyridine ring and an additional coordinating group (e.g., pyridine-3-carboxylic acid) tend to result in high yield. These heterocycles can coordinate with the metal center, enhancing catalytic activity.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with formyl groups and no additional stabilizing groups (e.g., 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid) are associated with low yield. The formyl group may lead to competing side reactions or deactivate the catalyst.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with a simple aliphatic chain and a terminal carboxylic acid, lacking additional functional groups (e.g., octanoic acid), tend to result in low yield. The lack of additional functional groups may limit interactions with the catalyst.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with both an amino and a carboxylic acid group but lacking aromatic or heterocyclic structures (e.g., 8-aminooctanoic acid) generally lead to low yield. The absence of aromatic stabilization may reduce the effectiveness of metal coordination.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group and an electron-withdrawing group such as a nitro group (e.g., 4-nitrobenzoic acid) tend to result in high yield. The electron-withdrawing group can enhance the acidity of the carboxylic acid, improving coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and a carboxylic acid group (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid) tend to result in low yield if there are no additional stabilizing groups. The lack of additional stabilizing groups may lead to insufficient coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a benzofuran structure and a carboxylic acid group (e.g., 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid) tend to result in high yield. The benzofuran structure can provide additional π-π interactions and stabilization.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and an electron-donating group such as an amino group (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid) tend to result in high yield. The electron-donating group enhances coordination with the metal center, increasing catalytic efficiency.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group and a methoxy group on an aromatic ring (e.g., 4-(3,4-dimethoxyphenyl)-4-oxobutanoic acid) tend to result in high yield. The methoxy group can enhance electron density and stabilization through resonance.

Rule Generator Message:
**Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing an amino acid structure with a carboxylic acid group and an aromatic ring, specifically phenyl or indole (e.g., (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid, (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid), tend to result in high yield. These structures enhance coordination with metal centers and stabilize the catalytic site through π-π interactions.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group directly attached to a carboxylic acid and an electron-donating group such as amino or hydroxyl (e.g., 4-aminobenzoic acid, 4-hydroxybenzoic acid) generally lead to high yield. The electron-donating groups increase electron density around the metal center, enhancing catalytic activity.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a hydroxyl group in conjunction with a carboxylic acid and an additional aromatic ring (e.g., 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid) are associated with high yield. The hydroxyl group enhances solubility and interaction with the catalyst, while the aromatic ring provides additional stabilization.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a sulfur-containing group, such as thiol or sulfanyl, and an aromatic ring (e.g., 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid) tend to result in high yield. Sulfur acts as a soft base, coordinating well with transition metals, and the aromatic ring stabilizes the active site.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with multiple ether linkages and no additional functional groups (e.g., 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid) tend to result in low yield. The ether groups may interfere with the coordination of the modifier to the metal center, reducing catalytic efficiency.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with a high degree of fluorination and no additional coordinating groups (e.g., 2,2,3,3,4,4,5,5,5-nonafluoropentanoic acid) generally lead to low yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of fluorine can destabilize the metal-ligand interaction.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with an indole or pyridine ring and an additional coordinating group (e.g., pyridine-3-carboxylic acid) tend to result in high yield. These heterocycles can coordinate with the metal center, enhancing catalytic activity.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with formyl groups and no additional stabilizing groups (e.g., 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid) are associated with low yield. The formyl group may lead to competing side reactions or deactivate the catalyst.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with a simple aliphatic chain and a terminal carboxylic acid, lacking additional functional groups (e.g., octanoic acid), tend to result in low yield. The lack of additional functional groups may limit interactions with the catalyst.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with both an amino and a carboxylic acid group but lacking aromatic or heterocyclic structures (e.g., 8-aminooctanoic acid) generally lead to low yield. The absence of aromatic stabilization may reduce the effectiveness of metal coordination.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group and an electron-withdrawing group such as a nitro group (e.g., 4-nitrobenzoic acid) tend to result in high yield. The electron-withdrawing group can enhance the acidity of the carboxylic acid, improving coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and a carboxylic acid group (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid) tend to result in low yield if there are no additional stabilizing groups. The lack of additional stabilizing groups may lead to insufficient coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a benzofuran structure and a carboxylic acid group (e.g., 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid) tend to result in high yield. The benzofuran structure can provide additional π-π interactions and stabilization.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and an electron-donating group such as an amino group (e.g., 4-pyridin-4-ylbutanoic acid) tend to result in high yield. The electron-donating group enhances coordination with the metal center, increasing catalytic efficiency.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group and a methoxy group on an aromatic ring (e.g., 4-(3,4-dimethoxyphenyl)-4-oxobutanoic acid) tend to result in high yield. The methoxy group can enhance electron density and stabilization through resonance.

ML Commenter Message:
### Performance Evaluation of Current Rules

1. **Effectiveness of Current Rules:**
   - The current validation accuracy of 0.6286 is lower than the previous reference accuracies, which were consistently around 0.76. This suggests that the current rules may not be as effective in capturing the underlying patterns in the data as the previous iterations.

2. **Overfitting/Underfitting Assessment:**
   - The drop in validation accuracy compared to previous iterations indicates potential underfitting. The model may not be capturing the complexity of the data adequately, possibly due to overly simplistic or misaligned rules.

3. **Discrepancies Analysis:**
   - For molecules where the predicted loading is high but the experimental value is low, Rule 1 and Rule 8 often have high positive SHAP values. This suggests that these rules might be contributing to overestimations.
   - Conversely, for molecules where the predicted loading is low but the experimental value is high, Rule 2 and Rule 8 frequently have high negative SHAP values, indicating potential underestimation issues.

4. **Accuracy Improvements:**
   - The current accuracy metrics suggest that there is room for improvement. The lower accuracy compared to previous iterations indicates that the current rules may not be capturing the necessary features effectively.

5. **Insights from SHAP Analysis:**
   - SHAP analysis highlights that Rule 1 and Rule 8 are significant contributors to both overestimations and underestimations. This suggests that these rules may need refinement to better align with the experimental data.

### Recommendations for Improvement

1. **Refine Rules with High Positive SHAP Values:**
   - **Rule 1:** Consider revisiting the conditions under which amino acid structures with carboxylic acid groups and aromatic rings are deemed to result in high yield. It may be beneficial to incorporate additional structural or electronic factors that could influence the interaction with the catalyst.
   - **Rule 8:** Re-evaluate the impact of formyl groups. Consider whether additional stabilizing groups or specific structural contexts could mitigate the negative impact of formyl groups on yield.

2. **Adjust Rules with High Negative SHAP Values:**
   - **Rule 2:** Investigate the conditions under which phenyl groups with electron-donating groups lead to high yield. It may be necessary to refine the rule to account for specific electronic or steric effects that could influence catalytic activity.
   - **Rule 8:** As mentioned, further analysis of the formyl group's role and potential interactions with other functional groups could help address underestimation issues.

3. **Improve Generalization:**
   - Consider incorporating additional features or modifying existing rules to capture more complex interactions between modifiers and catalysts. This could involve integrating more nuanced chemical descriptors or machine learning techniques to enhance rule generalization.

4. **Iterative Refinement:**
   - Use insights from SHAP analysis to iteratively test and refine rules. Focus on rules that consistently show high SHAP values, both positive and negative, to ensure they are accurately capturing the relevant chemical interactions.

By addressing these areas, the rules can be refined to improve model accuracy and better align predictions with experimental outcomes.

### Summary and Directions for Optimization

1. **Current Rule Performance:**
   - The current rules have shown improvements in confidence and lift, indicating better precision and accuracy in predicting high yield outcomes. However, the validation accuracy has decreased compared to previous iterations, suggesting potential underfitting.

3. **SHAP Analysis Insights:**
   - Rules 1 and 8 have high positive SHAP values, contributing to overestimations, while Rules 2 and 8 have high negative SHAP values, leading to underestimations. These rules may need refinement to better align with experimental data.

### Recommendations for Improvement

1. **Refinement of Specific Rules:**
   - **Rule 1:** Revisit the conditions for amino acid structures with carboxylic acid groups and aromatic rings. Consider additional structural or electronic factors that influence catalyst interaction.
   - **Rule 8:** Re-evaluate the impact of formyl groups and consider additional stabilizing groups or specific structural contexts to mitigate negative impacts.

2. **Adjust Rules with High Negative SHAP Values:**

ML Commenter Message:
### Performance Evaluation of Current Rules

1. **Effectiveness of Current Rules:**
   - The current rules have shown an improvement in validation accuracy from previous iterations, reaching 0.714 in the latest iteration. This suggests that the rules are becoming more effective in predicting the loading of modifiers on catalysts.

2. **Overfitting/Underfitting Assessment:**
   - The validation accuracy has improved over iterations, indicating that the model is learning and potentially reducing underfitting. However, the test accuracy is not provided for the latest iteration, which makes it difficult to fully assess overfitting. It would be beneficial to compare the validation and test accuracies to ensure the model generalizes well to unseen data.

3. **Discrepancies Analysis:**
   - For molecules where the predicted loading is high but the experimental value is low, rules with high positive SHAP values should be scrutinized. For instance, Rule 10 has a high positive SHAP value for some molecules with high predicted loadings but low experimental values, suggesting it might be overestimating the effect of certain features.
   - Conversely, for molecules where the predicted loading is low but the experimental value is high, rules with high negative SHAP values should be examined. Rule 3, for example, has a high negative SHAP value for some molecules with low predicted loadings but high experimental values, indicating it might be underestimating the effect of certain features.

4. **Accuracy Improvements:**
   - The increase in validation accuracy suggests that the rules are improving in capturing the underlying patterns in the data. The iterative refinement of rules appears to be moving towards a more optimal solution, although further iterations and test set evaluations are necessary to confirm this.

5. **Insights from SHAP Analysis:**
   - SHAP analysis highlights specific rules that contribute significantly to the predictions. For instance, Rule 1 and Rule 10 have high positive SHAP values for high predicted loadings, indicating their strong influence. Adjusting these rules to better align with experimental data could improve prediction accuracy.
   - Rules with consistently high negative SHAP values for underestimated loadings, such as Rule 3, may need to be adjusted to better capture the positive effects of certain features.

    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing an amino acid structure with a carboxylic acid group and an aromatic ring, specifically phenyl or indole, tend to result in high yield. The presence of these structures enhances coordination with metal centers and stabilizes the catalytic site through π-π interactions. Consider steric hindrance and electronic effects for better prediction.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group directly attached to a carboxylic acid and an electron-donating group such as amino or hydroxyl generally lead to high yield. The electron-donating groups increase electron density around the metal center, enhancing catalytic activity. Analyze substituent positions for improved accuracy.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a hydroxyl group in conjunction with a carboxylic acid and an additional aromatic ring are associated with high yield. The hydroxyl group enhances solubility and interaction with the catalyst, while the aromatic ring provides additional stabilization.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a sulfur-containing group, such as thiol or sulfanyl, and an aromatic ring tend to result in high yield. Sulfur acts as a soft base, coordinating well with transition metals, and the aromatic ring stabilizes the active site. Consider the position of sulfur-containing groups relative to aromatic rings for better prediction.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with multiple ether linkages and no additional functional groups tend to result in low yield. The ether groups may interfere with the coordination of the modifier to the metal center, reducing catalytic efficiency.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with a high degree of fluorination and no additional coordinating groups generally lead to low yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of fluorine can destabilize the metal-ligand interaction.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with an indole or pyridine ring and an additional coordinating group tend to result in high yield. These heterocycles can coordinate with the metal center, enhancing catalytic activity.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with formyl groups and no additional stabilizing groups are associated with low yield. Consider the presence of additional stabilizing groups or specific structural motifs that could mitigate the negative effects of formyl groups.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with a simple aliphatic chain and a terminal carboxylic acid, lacking additional functional groups, tend to result in low yield. However, if the aliphatic chain is sufficiently long or branched, it may lead to higher yield due to increased hydrophobic interactions.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with both an amino and a carboxylic acid group but lacking aromatic or heterocyclic structures generally lead to low yield. The absence of aromatic stabilization may reduce the effectiveness of metal coordination.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group and an electron-withdrawing group such as a nitro group tend to result in high yield. The electron-withdrawing group can enhance the acidity of the carboxylic acid, improving coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and a carboxylic acid group tend to result in low yield if there are no additional stabilizing groups. The lack of additional stabilizing groups may lead to insufficient coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a benzofuran structure and a carboxylic acid group tend to result in high yield. The benzofuran structure can provide additional π-π interactions and stabilization.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and an electron-donating group such as an amino group tend to result in high yield. The electron-donating group enhances coordination with the metal center, increasing catalytic efficiency.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group and a methoxy group on an aromatic ring tend to result in high yield. The methoxy group can enhance electron density and stabilization through resonance.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers with a sulfur-containing group, such as thiol or sulfanyl, and an aromatic ring tend to result in high yield. Sulfur acts as a soft base, coordinating well with transition metals, and the aromatic ring stabilizes the active site. Consider the position of sulfur-containing groups relative to aromatic rings for better prediction.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with multiple ether linkages and no additional functional groups tend to result in low yield. The ether groups may interfere with the coordination of the modifier to the metal center, reducing catalytic efficiency.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with a high degree of fluorination and no additional coordinating groups generally lead to low yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of fluorine can destabilize the metal-ligand interaction.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with an indole or pyridine ring and an additional coordinating group tend to result in high yield. These heterocycles can coordinate with the metal center, enhancing catalytic activity.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with formyl groups and no additional stabilizing groups are associated with low yield. Consider the presence of additional stabilizing groups or specific structural motifs that could mitigate the negative effects of formyl groups.

The absence of aromatic stabilization may reduce the effectiveness of metal coordination. - **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group and a methoxy group on an aromatic ring tend to result in high yield. The methoxy group can enhance electron density and stabilization through resonance.

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing an amino acid structure with a carboxylic acid group and an aromatic ring, specifically phenyl or indole, tend to result in high yield. These structures enhance coordination with metal centers and stabilize the catalytic site through π-π interactions. Consider steric hindrance and electronic effects for better prediction.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group directly attached to a carboxylic acid and an electron-donating group such as amino or hydroxyl generally lead to high yield. The electron-donating groups increase electron density around the metal center, enhancing catalytic activity. Analyze substituent positions for improved accuracy.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers with a sulfur-containing group, such as thiol or sulfanyl, and an aromatic ring tend to result in high yield. Sulfur acts as a soft base, coordinating well with transition metals, and the aromatic ring stabilizes the active site. Consider the position of sulfur-containing groups relative to aromatic rings for better prediction.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with multiple ether linkages and no additional functional groups tend to result in low yield. The ether groups may interfere with the coordination of the modifier to the metal center, reducing catalytic efficiency.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with a high degree of fluorination and no additional coordinating groups generally lead to low yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of fluorine can destabilize the metal-ligand interaction.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with an indole or pyridine ring and an additional coordinating group tend to result in high yield. These heterocycles can coordinate with the metal center, enhancing catalytic activity.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with formyl groups and no additional stabilizing groups are associated with low yield. Consider the presence of additional stabilizing groups or specific structural motifs that could mitigate the negative effects of formyl groups.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a simple aliphatic chain and a terminal carboxylic acid, lacking additional functional groups, tend to result in low yield. However, if the aliphatic chain is sufficiently long or branched, it may lead to higher yield due to increased hydrophobic interactions.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with both an amino and a carboxylic acid group but lacking aromatic or heterocyclic structures generally lead to low yield. The absence of aromatic stabilization may reduce the effectiveness of metal coordination. Consider the potential impact of chain length or branching in aliphatic chains, as these could enhance hydrophobic interactions and improve yield.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group and an electron-withdrawing group such as a nitro group tend to result in high yield. The electron-withdrawing group can enhance the acidity of the carboxylic acid, improving coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and a carboxylic acid group tend to result in low yield if there are no additional stabilizing groups. The lack of additional stabilizing groups may lead to insufficient coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a benzofuran structure and a carboxylic acid group tend to result in high yield. The benzofuran structure can provide additional π-π interactions and stabilization.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and an electron-donating group such as an amino group tend to result in high yield. The electron-donating group enhances coordination with the metal center, increasing catalytic efficiency.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group and a methoxy group on an aromatic ring tend to result in high yield. The methoxy group can enhance electron density and stabilization through resonance.

    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing an amino acid structure with a carboxylic acid group and an aromatic ring, specifically phenyl or indole, tend to result in high yield. These structures enhance coordination with metal centers and stabilize the catalytic site through π-π interactions. Consider steric hindrance and electronic effects for better prediction.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group directly attached to a carboxylic acid and an electron-donating group such as amino or hydroxyl generally lead to high yield. The electron-donating groups increase electron density around the metal center, enhancing catalytic activity. Analyze substituent positions for improved accuracy.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers with a sulfur-containing group, such as thiol or sulfanyl, and an aromatic ring tend to result in high yield. Sulfur acts as a soft base, coordinating well with transition metals, and the aromatic ring stabilizes the active site. Consider the position of sulfur-containing groups relative to aromatic rings for better prediction.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with multiple ether linkages and no additional functional groups tend to result in low yield. The ether groups may interfere with the coordination of the modifier to the metal center, reducing catalytic efficiency.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with a high degree of fluorination and no additional coordinating groups generally lead to low yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of fluorine can destabilize the metal-ligand interaction.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with an indole or pyridine ring and an additional coordinating group tend to result in high yield. These heterocycles can coordinate with the metal center, enhancing catalytic activity.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with formyl groups and no additional stabilizing groups are associated with low yield. Consider the presence of additional stabilizing groups or specific structural motifs that could mitigate the negative effects of formyl groups.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a simple aliphatic chain and a terminal carboxylic acid, lacking additional functional groups, tend to result in low yield. However, if the aliphatic chain is sufficiently long or branched, it may lead to higher yield due to increased hydrophobic interactions.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with both an amino and a carboxylic acid group but lacking aromatic or heterocyclic structures generally lead to low yield. The absence of aromatic stabilization may reduce the effectiveness of metal coordination. Consider the potential impact of chain length or branching in aliphatic chains, as these could enhance hydrophobic interactions and improve yield.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group and an electron-withdrawing group such as a nitro group tend to result in high yield. The electron-withdrawing group can enhance the acidity of the carboxylic acid, improving coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and a carboxylic acid group tend to result in low yield if there are no additional stabilizing groups. The lack of additional stabilizing groups may lead to insufficient coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a benzofuran structure and a carboxylic acid group tend to result in high yield. The benzofuran structure can provide additional π-π interactions and stabilization.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and an electron-donating group such as an amino group tend to result in high yield. The electron-donating group enhances coordination with the metal center, increasing catalytic efficiency.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group and a methoxy group on an aromatic ring tend to result in high yield. The methoxy group can enhance electron density and stabilization through resonance.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a combination of a pyridine ring, a carboxylic acid group, and an additional electron-donating group such as hydroxyl or amino tend to result in high yield. The combination of these groups can enhance coordination and stabilization of the metal center.

Rule Generator Message:
**Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing an amino acid structure with a carboxylic acid group and an aromatic ring, specifically phenyl or indole, tend to result in high yield. These structures enhance coordination with metal centers and stabilize the catalytic site through π-π interactions. Consider steric hindrance and electronic effects for better prediction.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group directly attached to a carboxylic acid and an electron-donating group such as amino or hydroxyl generally lead to high yield. The electron-donating groups increase electron density around the metal center, enhancing catalytic activity. Analyze substituent positions for improved accuracy.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers with a sulfur-containing group, such as thiol or sulfanyl, and an aromatic ring tend to result in high yield. Sulfur acts as a soft base, coordinating well with transition metals, and the aromatic ring stabilizes the active site. Consider the position of sulfur-containing groups relative to aromatic rings for better prediction.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with multiple ether linkages and no additional functional groups tend to result in low yield. The ether groups may interfere with the coordination of the modifier to the metal center, reducing catalytic efficiency.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with a high degree of fluorination and no additional coordinating groups generally lead to low yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of fluorine can destabilize the metal-ligand interaction.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with an indole or pyridine ring and an additional coordinating group tend to result in high yield. These heterocycles can coordinate with the metal center, enhancing catalytic activity.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with formyl groups and no additional stabilizing groups are associated with low yield. Consider the presence of additional stabilizing groups or specific structural motifs that could mitigate the negative effects of formyl groups.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a simple aliphatic chain and a terminal carboxylic acid, lacking additional functional groups, tend to result in low yield. However, if the aliphatic chain is sufficiently long or branched, it may lead to higher yield due to increased hydrophobic interactions.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with both an amino and a carboxylic acid group but lacking aromatic or heterocyclic structures generally lead to low yield. The absence of aromatic stabilization may reduce the effectiveness of metal coordination. Consider the potential impact of chain length or branching in aliphatic chains, as these could enhance hydrophobic interactions and improve yield.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group and an electron-withdrawing group such as a nitro group tend to result in high yield. The electron-withdrawing group can enhance the acidity of the carboxylic acid, improving coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and a carboxylic acid group tend to result in low yield if there are no additional stabilizing groups. The lack of additional stabilizing groups may lead to insufficient coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a benzofuran structure and a carboxylic acid group tend to result in high yield. The benzofuran structure can provide additional π-π interactions and stabilization.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and an electron-donating group such as an amino group tend to result in high yield. The electron-donating group enhances coordination with the metal center, increasing catalytic efficiency.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group and a methoxy group on an aromatic ring tend to result in high yield. The methoxy group can enhance electron density and stabilization through resonance.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a combination of a pyridine ring, a carboxylic acid group, and an additional electron-donating group such as hydroxyl or amino tend to result in high yield. The combination of these groups can enhance coordination and stabilization of the metal center.

**End of Rules**

4. **Support**: The current train support is 0.0267, which is lower than the previous train support values (0.1667 and 0.1845) and test support values (0.0833 and 0.1354). ML Commenter Message:
### Performance Evaluation of Current Rules

1. **Effectiveness of Current Rules:**
   - The current rules have shown some effectiveness, as indicated by the validation accuracy of 0.6286. However, this is a decrease from the previous reference accuracies, which were consistently around 0.76. This suggests that the current rules may not be capturing the underlying patterns as effectively as before.

2. **Overfitting/Underfitting:**
   - The decrease in validation accuracy suggests potential underfitting, where the rules are not complex enough to capture the nuances of the data. The consistent validation accuracy across iterations indicates that the model might be stuck in a local minimum, unable to improve further with the current rule set.

3. **Discrepancies Analysis:**
   - For molecules where the predicted loading is high but the experimental value is low, rules with high positive SHAP values (e.g., Rule 2 and Rule 9) might be overestimating the effect of certain features.
   - Conversely, for molecules where the predicted loading is low but the experimental value is high, rules with high negative SHAP values (e.g., Rule 7 and Rule 11) might be underestimating the effect of certain features.

4. **Accuracy Improvements:**
   - The current accuracy metrics suggest limited improvement potential with the existing rules. The model's performance has plateaued, indicating that the rules may need significant refinement or expansion to capture more complex interactions.

5. **Insights from SHAP Analysis:**
   - SHAP analysis indicates that certain rules, such as Rule 2 and Rule 9, have a strong influence on predictions. These rules may need to be refined to prevent overestimation.
   - Rules like Rule 7 and Rule 11, which consistently show negative SHAP values, might need adjustments to better capture the conditions under which they should predict high loading.

### Recommendations for Improvement

1. **Refine Overestimated Rules:**
   - **Rule 2**: Consider the specific positions and types of electron-donating groups. The rule might be too broad, leading to overestimation. Narrowing down the conditions under which these groups enhance yield could improve accuracy.
   - **Rule 9**: Re-evaluate the impact of chain length and branching in aliphatic chains. This rule might be overestimating the negative impact of lacking aromatic structures.

2. **Adjust Underestimated Rules:**
   - **Rule 7**: Investigate additional stabilizing groups that could mitigate the negative effects of formyl groups. This rule might be underestimating the potential for high yield in the presence of other stabilizing features.
   - **Rule 11**: Consider additional stabilizing groups that could enhance coordination with the metal center, potentially increasing yield.

3. **Improve Generalization:**
   - Introduce more nuanced rules that consider the interplay between different functional groups and their positions. This could help address underfitting by capturing more complex patterns in the data.

4. **Iterative Refinement:**
   - Use insights from SHAP analysis to iteratively refine the rules. Focus on rules with consistently high positive or negative SHAP values and adjust them to better align with experimental outcomes.

5. **Expand Rule Set:**
   - Consider adding new rules that capture interactions not currently covered, such as the impact of specific heterocycles or the role of steric hindrance in modifying catalytic activity.

By implementing these recommendations, the rules can be refined to improve both the accuracy and generalization of the model, potentially leading to better alignment with experimental results.

The electron-donating groups increase electron density around the metal center, enhancing catalytic activity. Analyze substituent positions for improved accuracy.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers with a sulfur-containing group, such as thiol or sulfanyl, and an aromatic ring tend to result in high yield. Sulfur acts as a soft base, coordinating well with transition metals, and the aromatic ring stabilizes the active site. Consider the position of sulfur-containing groups relative to aromatic rings for better prediction.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with multiple ether linkages and no additional functional groups tend to result in low yield. The ether groups may interfere with the coordination of the modifier to the metal center, reducing catalytic efficiency.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with a high degree of fluorination and no additional coordinating groups generally lead to low yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of fluorine can destabilize the metal-ligand interaction.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with an indole or pyridine ring and an additional coordinating group tend to result in high yield. These heterocycles can coordinate with the metal center, enhancing catalytic activity.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with formyl groups and no additional stabilizing groups are associated with low yield. Consider the presence of additional stabilizing groups or specific structural motifs that could mitigate the negative effects of formyl groups.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a simple aliphatic chain and a terminal carboxylic acid, lacking additional functional groups, tend to result in low yield. However, if the aliphatic chain is sufficiently long or branched, it may lead to higher yield due to increased hydrophobic interactions.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with both an amino and a carboxylic acid group but lacking aromatic or heterocyclic structures generally lead to low yield. The absence of aromatic stabilization may reduce the effectiveness of metal coordination. Consider the potential impact of chain length or branching in aliphatic chains, as these could enhance hydrophobic interactions and improve yield.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group and an electron-withdrawing group such as a nitro group tend to result in high yield. The electron-withdrawing group can enhance the acidity of the carboxylic acid, improving coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and a carboxylic acid group tend to result in low yield if there are no additional stabilizing groups. The lack of additional stabilizing groups may lead to insufficient coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a benzofuran structure and a carboxylic acid group tend to result in high yield. The benzofuran structure can provide additional π-π interactions and stabilization.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and an electron-donating group such as an amino group tend to result in high yield. The electron-donating group enhances coordination with the metal center, increasing catalytic efficiency.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group and a methoxy group on an aromatic ring tend to result in high yield. The methoxy group can enhance electron density and stabilization through resonance.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a combination of a pyridine ring, a carboxylic acid group, and an additional electron-donating group such as hydroxyl or amino tend to result in high yield. The combination of these groups can enhance coordination and stabilization of the metal center.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group directly attached to a carboxylic acid and an electron-donating group such as amino or hydroxyl generally lead to high yield. The electron-donating groups increase electron density around the metal center, enhancing catalytic activity. Analyze substituent positions for improved accuracy.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers with a sulfur-containing group, such as thiol or sulfanyl, and an aromatic ring tend to result in high yield. Sulfur acts as a soft base, coordinating well with transition metals, and the aromatic ring stabilizes the active site. Consider the position of sulfur-containing groups relative to aromatic rings for better prediction.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with multiple ether linkages and no additional functional groups tend to result in low yield. The ether groups may interfere with the coordination of the modifier to the metal center, reducing catalytic efficiency.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with a high degree of fluorination and no additional coordinating groups generally lead to low yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of fluorine can destabilize the metal-ligand interaction.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with an indole or pyridine ring and an additional coordinating group tend to result in high yield. These heterocycles can coordinate with the metal center, enhancing catalytic activity.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with formyl groups and no additional stabilizing groups are associated with low yield. Consider the presence of additional stabilizing groups or specific structural motifs that could mitigate the negative effects of formyl groups.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a simple aliphatic chain and a terminal carboxylic acid, lacking additional functional groups, tend to result in low yield. However, if the aliphatic chain is sufficiently long or branched, it may lead to higher yield due to increased hydrophobic interactions.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with both an amino and a carboxylic acid group but lacking aromatic or heterocyclic structures generally lead to low yield. The absence of aromatic stabilization may reduce the effectiveness of metal coordination. Consider the potential impact of chain length or branching in aliphatic chains, as these could enhance hydrophobic interactions and improve yield.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group and an electron-withdrawing group such as a nitro group tend to result in high yield. The electron-withdrawing group can enhance the acidity of the carboxylic acid, improving coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and a carboxylic acid group tend to result in low yield if there are no additional stabilizing groups. The lack of additional stabilizing groups may lead to insufficient coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a benzofuran structure and a carboxylic acid group tend to result in high yield. The benzofuran structure can provide additional π-π interactions and stabilization.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and an electron-donating group such as an amino group tend to result in high yield. The electron-donating group enhances coordination with the metal center, increasing catalytic efficiency.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group and a methoxy group on an aromatic ring tend to result in high yield. The methoxy group can enhance electron density and stabilization through resonance.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a combination of a pyridine ring, a carboxylic acid group, and an additional electron-donating group such as hydroxyl or amino tend to result in high yield. The combination of these groups can enhance coordination and stabilization of the metal center.

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing an amino acid structure with a carboxylic acid group and an aromatic ring, specifically phenyl or indole, tend to result in high yield. These structures enhance coordination with metal centers and stabilize the catalytic site through π-π interactions. Consider steric hindrance and electronic effects for better prediction.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group directly attached to a carboxylic acid and an electron-donating group such as amino or hydroxyl generally lead to high yield. The electron-donating groups increase electron density around the metal center, enhancing catalytic activity. Analyze substituent positions for improved accuracy.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers with a sulfur-containing group, such as thiol or sulfanyl, and an aromatic ring tend to result in high yield. Sulfur acts as a soft base, coordinating well with transition metals, and the aromatic ring stabilizes the active site. Consider the position of sulfur-containing groups relative to aromatic rings for better prediction.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with multiple ether linkages and no additional functional groups tend to result in low yield. The ether groups may interfere with the coordination of the modifier to the metal center, reducing catalytic efficiency.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with a high degree of fluorination and no additional coordinating groups generally lead to low yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of fluorine can destabilize the metal-ligand interaction.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with an indole or pyridine ring and an additional coordinating group tend to result in high yield. These heterocycles can coordinate with the metal center, enhancing catalytic activity.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with formyl groups and no additional stabilizing groups are associated with low yield. Consider the presence of additional stabilizing groups or specific structural motifs that could mitigate the negative effects of formyl groups.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a simple aliphatic chain and a terminal carboxylic acid, lacking additional functional groups, tend to result in low yield. However, if the aliphatic chain is sufficiently long or branched, it may lead to higher yield due to increased hydrophobic interactions.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with both an amino and a carboxylic acid group but lacking aromatic or heterocyclic structures generally lead to low yield. The absence of aromatic stabilization may reduce the effectiveness of metal coordination. Consider the potential impact of chain length or branching in aliphatic chains, as these could enhance hydrophobic interactions and improve yield.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group and an electron-withdrawing group such as a nitro group tend to result in high yield. The electron-withdrawing group can enhance the acidity of the carboxylic acid, improving coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and a carboxylic acid group tend to result in low yield if there are no additional stabilizing groups. The lack of additional stabilizing groups may lead to insufficient coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a benzofuran structure and a carboxylic acid group tend to result in high yield. The benzofuran structure can provide additional π-π interactions and stabilization.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and an electron-donating group such as an amino group tend to result in high yield. The electron-donating group enhances coordination with the metal center, increasing catalytic efficiency.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group and a methoxy group on an aromatic ring tend to result in high yield. The methoxy group can enhance electron density and stabilization through resonance.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a combination of a pyridine ring, a carboxylic acid group, and an additional electron-donating group such as hydroxyl or amino tend to result in high yield. The combination of these groups can enhance coordination and stabilization of the metal center.

    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing an amino acid structure with a carboxylic acid group and an aromatic ring, specifically phenyl or indole, tend to result in high yield. These structures enhance coordination with metal centers and stabilize the catalytic site through π-π interactions. Consider steric hindrance and electronic effects for better prediction.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl group directly attached to a carboxylic acid and an electron-donating group such as amino or hydroxyl generally lead to high yield. The electron-donating groups increase electron density around the metal center, enhancing catalytic activity. Analyze substituent positions for improved accuracy.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers with a sulfur-containing group, such as thiol or sulfanyl, and an aromatic ring tend to result in high yield. Sulfur acts as a soft base, coordinating well with transition metals, and the aromatic ring stabilizes the active site. Consider the position of sulfur-containing groups relative to aromatic rings for better prediction.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with multiple ether linkages and no additional functional groups tend to result in low yield. The ether groups may interfere with the coordination of the modifier to the metal center, reducing catalytic efficiency.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with a high degree of fluorination and no additional coordinating groups generally lead to low yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of fluorine can destabilize the metal-ligand interaction.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with an indole or pyridine ring and an additional coordinating group tend to result in high yield. These heterocycles can coordinate with the metal center, enhancing catalytic activity.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with formyl groups and no additional stabilizing groups are associated with low yield. Consider the presence of additional stabilizing groups or specific structural motifs that could mitigate the negative effects of formyl groups.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers with a simple aliphatic chain and a terminal carboxylic acid, lacking additional functional groups, tend to result in low yield. However, if the aliphatic chain is sufficiently long or branched, it may lead to higher yield due to increased hydrophobic interactions.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with both an amino and a carboxylic acid group but lacking aromatic or heterocyclic structures generally lead to low yield. The absence of aromatic stabilization may reduce the effectiveness of metal coordination. Consider the potential impact of chain length or branching in aliphatic chains, as these could enhance hydrophobic interactions and improve yield.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group and an electron-withdrawing group such as a nitro group tend to result in high yield. The electron-withdrawing group can enhance the acidity of the carboxylic acid, improving coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and a carboxylic acid group tend to result in low yield if there are no additional stabilizing groups. The lack of additional stabilizing groups may lead to insufficient coordination with the metal center.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a benzofuran structure and a carboxylic acid group tend to result in high yield. The benzofuran structure can provide additional π-π interactions and stabilization.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and an electron-donating group such as an amino group tend to result in high yield. The electron-donating group enhances coordination with the metal center, increasing catalytic efficiency.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group and a methoxy group on an aromatic ring tend to result in high yield. The methoxy group can enhance electron density and stabilization through resonance.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a combination of a pyridine ring, a carboxylic acid group, and an additional electron-donating group such as hydroxyl or amino tend to result in high yield. The combination of these groups can enhance coordination and stabilization of the metal center.
